    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fc091f2a4d6341459e1a5c0d0c70f50b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'cd4e4f44-6a53-4625-b84d-cc3f5f8a616d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Department of Chemistry and Shanghai Key Laboratory of Molecular Catalysis and Innovative Materials, Institute of New Energy, Laboratory of Advanced Materials, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200438 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Yonggang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Dong/Xiaoli" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaoli Dong<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d69773537" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) hold tremendous potential in next-generation energy storage. However, no SIB has yet achieved simultaneous support for high voltage, rapid charging, and all-climate adaptability due to electrolyte limitations. This study successfully constructs versatile SIBs using an optimized acetonitrile (AN)-based electrolyte, which offers excellent high-voltage tolerance, high ionic conductivity, anion-enriched solvation structure, and a wide liquidus temperature range. The engineered solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) exhibits low resistance and exceptional stability, effectively supporting fast charging, temperature-adaptive operation, and long-term cycling stability. Consequently, this tailored electrolyte, combined with a robust SEI, enables hard carbon (HC) anodes to achieve a high reversible capacity of 223 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a fast rate of 5&nbsp;C. When paired with a high-voltage NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode, the HC||NFM full cells operate stably at a high cut-off voltage of 4.15 V, sustaining over 1400 cycles at 5&nbsp;C. Furthermore, a practical 3 Ah pouch cell demonstrates excellent stability, retaining 90.7% of its capacity after 1000 cycles, and shows all-climate adaptability, maintaining 56.4% of its room-temperature capacity at −60&nbsp;°C and 97.3% retention after 350 cycles at 50&nbsp;°C. This work provides valuable insights for developing advanced electrolytes for versatile and practical SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405319-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a promising next-generation energy storage solution, owing to the abundance of sodium (Na) resources, lower costs, and reduced environmental impact, offering scalable and sustainable potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0001_R_d69773515e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their applications range from large-scale stationary energy storage, power tools, electric bicycles, and small electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0002_R_d69773515e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address diverse application scenarios, SIBs must satisfy key requirements, including high energy density, long cycle life, fast charging capability, and reliable performance across a wide range of environmental conditions. These demands place significant pressure on the three core components of SIBs: the cathode, anode, and electrolyte. Satisfactory cathode materials for SIBs, including layer-structured transition metal oxides, polyanionic compounds, and Prussian blue analogs, have already been extensively reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0003_R_d69773515e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Chin. Chem. Lett. Chin. Chem. Lett.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the anode, it is essential to have a material with low redox potential and high capacity to achieve high energy density when paired with high-voltage cathodes. Amorphous carbon-based materials, such as hard carbon (HC), provide high Na storage capacity, low Na storage potential (e.g., 0.1 V vs Na⁺/Na), and environmental sustainability, positioning them as leading candidates for SIB anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0004_R_d69773515e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - eScience Mater. Today Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, various highly efficient advanced HC materials have also been reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0005_R_d69773515e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Energy Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to electrode materials, the electrolyte—often described as the battery's “lifeblood”—plays a critical role in determining the battery's operating voltage range and temperature limits. In this context, practical SIBs still face significant challenges, primarily related to poor cycling stability, sluggish electrode-electrolyte interface kinetics, and inadequate temperature adaptability, largely stemming from limitations in the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The quality of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed by the electrolyte significantly affects sodium-ion (Na<sup>+</sup>) transport kinetics and interfacial stability, directly influencing low-temperature performance, fast-charging capability, and cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0006_R_d69773515e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the instability of the electrode-electrolyte interface leads to ongoing parasitic reactions, resulting in continuous capacity fade and limiting the battery's operational lifespan.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0007_R_d69773515e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At elevated temperatures (&gt; 45&nbsp;°C), these side reactions between the electrolyte and electrodes intensify, causing the gradual consumption of active Na and accelerating battery failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0008_R_d69773515e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - EcoMat">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, under high-rate or low-temperature conditions, polarization on HC with high interfacial impedance can induce Na plating, leading to electrolyte degradation and eventual battery failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0009_R_d69773515e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - eScience">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To meet the diverse application demands, advanced electrolytes must not only satisfy basic criteria such as low viscosity, high ionic conductivity, a wide electrochemical stability window, flame retardancy, and compatibility across a broad temperature range but also possess the ability to form a stable SEI on HC. Robust SEI is essential for preventing parasitic reactions while facilitating rapid Na⁺ transport kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>Commercial carbonate-based electrolytes exhibit good oxidative stability and have been widely used with high-voltage cathode materials. However, they tend to form high-impedance SEI films on HC, hindering fast charging and limiting low-temperature performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0010_R_d69773515e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, recent studies have shown that ether-based electrolytes can form high-quality SEI layers on HC,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0010_R_d69773515e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> enabling fast charging and extremely low-temperature applications (ca. ≤ −40&nbsp;°C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0011_R_d69773515e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, their poor oxidative stability restricts the selection of high-voltage cathode materials, thereby limiting the energy density of SIBs. Meanwhile, phosphate-based electrolytes, a more recent development, offer excellent flame retardancy but struggle to form a stable interface film. This limitation leads to a shorter cycle life and makes it challenging to support operations in extreme climates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0001_R_d69773515e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Nat. Energy">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0012_R_d69773515e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, current state-of-the-art electrolytes in SIBs—carbonates, ethers, and phosphates—do not fully meet all necessary performance criteria. This highlights the urgent need for a new electrolyte system with superior overall properties to enable the practical deployment of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we have employed a top-down approach to design a nitrile-based electrolyte specifically tailored for practical and versatile SIBs. Following careful consideration of viscosity, dielectric constant, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, transference number, electrochemical stability window, and desolvation energy, acetonitrile (AN) was ultimately selected as the base solvent. The significant improvement in the rate performance of HC in 1 mol L<sup>−1</sup> (M) sodium bis (fluoro sulfonyl) imide (NaFSI) in AN electrolyte suggests the effectiveness of the solvent screening strategy. To enhance interfacial stability, non-coordinating diluents were introduced to optimize the electrolyte composition, creating an anion-rich solvation structure that successfully forms a robust inorganic-rich SEI. This optimized electrolyte, characterized by high ionic conductivity, low desolvation energy, and a stable SEI, enables exceptional high-rate performance in HC electrodes. Notably, the HC electrode achieves a specific capacity of 223 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C, which is double that of traditional carbonate-based electrolytes. Additionally, the HC||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) full cell exhibits stable cycling over 2500 cycles at 5&nbsp;C, with a capacity retention of 79.1%. The high oxidative stability of AN also enhances compatibility with various cathode materials. For example, the HC||NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) full cell maintains 82.0% capacity retention after 1400 cycles at a high cut-off voltage of 4.15 V. Furthermore, this electrolyte has been validated in a 3 Ah pouch cell, demonstrating capacity retention of 90.7% after 1000 cycles at 1&nbsp;C, alongside reliable operation across a wide temperature range from −60 to 50&nbsp;°C. Specifically, it retains 81.0% of its capacity at −40&nbsp;°C, 56.4% at −60&nbsp;°C, and shows 97.3% capacity retention after 350 cycles at 50&nbsp;°C.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405319-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0030-title">2.1 Solvent-Screening</h3>
            
            <p>Viscosity is a critical factor influencing the transport properties of electrolytes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as ion diffusion, conductivity, and transference number. Achieving high ionic conductivity requires low viscosity, which is crucial for reducing ionic transport resistance, especially in batteries operating under high-rate and low-temperature conditions. Among the common solvents including ethylene carbonate (EC), propylene carbonate (PC), dimethyl carbonate (DMC), ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), tetrahydrofuran (THF), 1,3-dioxolane (DOL), ethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DME), diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DIG), trimethyl phosphate (TMP), and AN, cyclic carbonates typically exhibit higher viscosities, such as 1.9 cP for EC and 2.53 cP for PC (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0001">1a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such high viscosities significantly impair separator wettability and hinder Na<sup>+</sup> transport within the electrolyte despite their high dielectric constants. In contrast, solvents like DMC, THF, DME, TMP, and AN, which have lower viscosities, are more suitable for high-rate SIBs. Among these solvents, AN begins to stand out, not only for its lowest viscosity (i.e., 0.37) but also for its relatively high dielectric constant (i.e., 36.6),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0014_R_d69773515e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Rev.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which ensures efficient dissolution of conductive salts.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/614d66fe-6d20-4055-8d4b-333f77a8e943/aenm202405319-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/614d66fe-6d20-4055-8d4b-333f77a8e943/aenm202405319-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/269af667-b2a9-4861-9248-4e8e7e0da252/aenm202405319-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/614d66fe-6d20-4055-8d4b-333f77a8e943/aenm202405319-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405319-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405319" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrolyte design and solvent screening. a) Viscosity and dielectric constant of solvents. b) Na<sup>+</sup> transference number and diffusion coefficient of 1M NaFSI in different solvents obtained from molecular dynamics simulations. c) Oxidation potentials, reduction potentials, and electrochemical stability window of isolated solvent molecules obtained from DFT calculations. d) Binding energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Diffusion coefficients measure the rate at which Na<sup>+</sup> moves through the electrolyte, while the transference number indicates the proportion of current carried by Na<sup>+</sup>. Molecular dynamics simulations were used to predict these properties for electrolytes containing 1 M NaFSI dissolved in various solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0015_R_d69773515e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Rev.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The analysis indicates that although TMP exhibits low viscosity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0001">1a</a>), its Na<sup>+</sup> transport performance is relatively poor, with a Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of only 0.07&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and a transference number of just 0.35 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0001">1b</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), making it unsuitable for high-rate SIB applications. Ether-based solvents, in general, exhibited higher diffusion coefficients and transference numbers compared to carbonates. Among the ethers, DME and DOL stand out, with DME achieving the highest diffusion coefficient of 0.88&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and DOL the highest transference number of 0.52. These findings support the frequent use of ether-based electrolytes in high-rate SIBs, as they facilitate the rapid charging of HC electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0011_R_d69773515e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Noteworthy, AN-based electrolytes exhibited a high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 1.03&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and a high transference number of 0.54, both indicative of superior Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Prior studies have also shown that the ionic conductivity of the AN-based electrolyte is substantially higher than that of other commonly used rechargeable battery electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0016_R_d69773515e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The electrochemical stability of an electrolyte is a crucial parameter that dictates the range of compatible cathode materials. It is defined by the voltage window between the oxidation and reduction decomposition limits.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While ether-based solvents are effective for fast charging, they typically have a narrower electrochemical stability window and lower oxidative stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0001">1c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). For example, DME has an oxidation potential of 4.00 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and an electrochemical stability window of 4.30 V, making it less suitable for high-voltage applications. In comparison, AN exhibits the widest electrochemical stability window (ca. 6.03 V), with density functional theory (DFT) calculations indicating an oxidation potential of 6.50 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which is even much higher than the 5.48 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of DEC. Although there may be numerical differences between theoretical predictions and experimental data, they follow consistent trends.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0017_R_d69773515e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Phys. Chem. C Nat. Nanotechnol.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results of these theoretical predictions indicate that AN can effectively support high-voltage operation.</p>
            
            <p>Moreover, desolvation energy, a key factor for low-temperature batteries,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0018_R_d69773515e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was evaluated by analyzing the binding energies between Na<sup>+</sup> and various solvents. DMC, DOL, THF, and AN exhibited relatively weak binding energies with Na<sup>+</sup>, specifically −33.88, −29.23, −34.23, and −34.36 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0001">1d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, suggesting their potential for facilitating rapid desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> at the HC-electrolyte interface. This rapid desolvation is expected to enhance the performance of high-power and low-temperature SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0018_R_d69773515e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, weaker binding energy suggests reduced solvation capability, which is thought to facilitate the formation of an anion-derived SEI layer rich in inorganic species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0019_R_d69773515e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the operating temperature range of the electrolyte is a critical factor, as it typically defines the upper and lower limits of the battery's operational temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> AN, with its melting point of −43.8&nbsp;°C and boiling point of 81.6&nbsp;°C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0014_R_d69773515e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Rev.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> provides a broad liquid range, making it adaptable to various application scenarios. Moreover, AN-based electrolytes demonstrate remarkable flame retardancy, significantly shortening self-extinguishing times compared to commercial carbonate and conventional ether-based electrolytes. Specifically, while the DME-based electrolyte burned for 39.4 s (Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and the EC/DEC-based electrolyte for 36.5 s (Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the AN-based electrolyte self-extinguished in just 3.9 s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>; Video <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). This rapid self-extinguishing behavior offers a crucial advantage for improving battery safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0020_R_d69773515e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In view of the above considerations, AN was identified as the optimal solvent due to its unique combination of advantageous properties, including low viscosity, relatively high dielectric constant, excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and transference, low desolvation energy, the widest electrochemical stability window, an appropriate liquid temperature range, and outstanding flame retardancy. These characteristics make AN exceptionally suitable for practical and versatile SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance of the Acetonitrile-Based Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the performance of the AN-based electrolyte, we first prepared a baseline electrolyte containing 1 M NaFSI in AN (denoted as 1M-AN) and examined the rate performance of HC, a critical limiting factor in high-power and low-temperature SIBs. The rate performance was assessed using a Na-metal-free three-electrode configuration, an approach particularly well-suited for HC-based SIBs as it eliminates the interference from highly reactive metallic Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0021_R_d69773515e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Sci. China Chem.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although HC demonstrated comparable reversible capacities in both carbonate (i.e., 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in EC/DEC (3:7 in volume ratio), denoted as NP186) and AN-based electrolytes at low rates—specifically 291 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in carbonate and 295 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in AN-based electrolyte at 0.2&nbsp;C—the distinction between the two became more evident at higher rates (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2a</a>). The AN-based electrolyte retained 79.7% of its 0.2&nbsp;C capacity, delivering 235 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;C and 66.8% capacity, with 197 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C. In comparison, the carbonate-based electrolyte retained only 60.5% of its 0.2&nbsp;C capacity at 3&nbsp;C, which corresponds to 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and just 38.1% at 5&nbsp;C, equating to 111 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2a</a>). Analysis of the HC charge-discharge profiles revealed significantly lower polarization in the AN-based electrolyte. Notably, the mid-point potential of HC during sodiation at 5&nbsp;C reached −3.086 V (vs 50SOC-NVP, equating to 0.294 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) in the carbonate electrolyte, significantly higher than the −3.212 V (vs 50SOC-NVP, equate to 0.168 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) observed in the 1M-AN electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2b</a>). Consequently, HC cycled in the carbonate electrolyte exhibited markedly lower plateau capacities at high rates, retaining only 52% at 3C and 41% at 5&nbsp;C, compared to 63% at 0.2&nbsp;C. Conversely, the AN-based electrolyte maintained a robust plateau capacity of 59% even at 5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2c</a>). The notable improvement in rate performance validates the effectiveness of the solvent screening strategy outlined in the previous section.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ae8fef3e-1b4c-4d74-8267-835d83cb2004/aenm202405319-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ae8fef3e-1b4c-4d74-8267-835d83cb2004/aenm202405319-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cb33ac68-acb5-4f39-bc6f-6a1ffa1a64d1/aenm202405319-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ae8fef3e-1b4c-4d74-8267-835d83cb2004/aenm202405319-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405319-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405319" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the AN-based electrolyte. a) The rate performance of HC obtained using a Na-metal-free three-electrode configuration, and b) mid-point potential during sodiation, c) capacity contribution from the slope and plateau regions. d) Long-term cycling performance of the HC||NVP full cell. e) In situ impedance variation of the HC electrode during cycling, and f) corresponding DRT analysis curves.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To gain insights into the cycling stability of the AN-based electrolyte, HC||NVP cells were assembled. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2d</a>, conventional carbonate-based&nbsp;NP186 electrolyte experienced a significant capacity loss after 500 cycles at 5&nbsp;C. This is likely due to the poor rate performance of HC, which resulted in repeated Na deposition, the formation of a high-impedance SEI, and electrolyte consumption (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), ultimately leading to nonlinear aging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0022_R_d69773515e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Energy Storage">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the AN-based electrolyte exhibited no such degradation, resulting in higher cycling stability. However, despite this advantage, the cycling performance of the electrolyte, composed solely of a single AN solvent and conventional concentrations of conductive salt, was suboptimal, retaining only 33.4% of its capacity after 2500 cycles.</p>
            
            <p>Previous studies have indicated that AN has a relatively high reducibility and fails to form a stable SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0013_R_d69773515e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nature Chem. Rev.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0023_R_d69773515e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In situ impedance measurements revealed a continuous increase in interfacial impedance during cycling with the 1M-AN electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2e</a>). In the distribution of relaxation times (DRT) spectrum, seven distinct time constants labeled P1 to P7 are observed. Among them, P1 is associated with the Ohmic resistance contributions from ionic resistance and electronic resistance of the electrolytes and electrodes. P7 corresponds to the diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup> within the HC electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0024_R_d69773515e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Storage Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intermediate time constants, P2 to P6, are linked to the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> at the HC interface, encompassing the impedance of various SEI components as well as the charge transfer impedance of Na<sup>+</sup> within the SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0025_R_d69773515e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem Joule">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The time constants P2 to P6, associated with interfacial impedance, increase progressively with the number of cycles, showing a clear upward trend in impedance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2f</a>). This indicates poor stability of the SEI layer derived from the 1M-AN electrolyte, as continuous interfacial side reactions lead to the thickening and growth of the SEI layer over the course of cycling.</p>
            
            <p>Common strategies to enhance the stability of the electrode-electrolyte interface involve either the use of film-forming additives or adjusting the concentration of conductive salts to promote anionic participation in film formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0026_R_d69773515e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The former typically involves introducing additional molecules as sacrificial film-forming additives that preferentially undergo reduction decomposition to form a stable SEI layer, thus suppressing the decomposition of existing electrolyte components and stabilizing the interface.</p>
            
            <p>In this study, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and sodium difluoro(oxalato)borate (NaDFOB) were introduced as film-forming additives, both of which are widely used in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0027_R_d69773515e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the addition of FEC or NaDFOB suppressed the decomposition of NaFSI. Notably, NaDFOB also helped inhibit the decomposition of AN, thereby significantly improving the initial Coulombic efficiency of the HC electrode. However, despite their similar ionic conductivity to the 1M-AN electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), the rate performance of the HC with these additive-containing electrolytes was significantly diminished. This suggests that the SEI formed by these additives is not suitable for high-power HC-based SIBs. These findings highlight the importance of not only optimizing ionic conductivity and desolvation energy but also creating interfaces conducive to rapid ion transport in high-rate SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0050-title">2.3 Solvation Structure Optimization</h3>
            
            <p>An alternative strategy for stabilizing the interface is to increase the concentration of conductive salts in the existing electrolyte or introduce a non-coordinating diluent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0023_R_d69773515e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0028_R_d69773515e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-concentration electrolytes and localized high-concentration electrolytes exemplify this approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0029_R_d69773515e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Electrochem">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this study, 1H,1H,5H-octafluoropentyl-1,1,2,2-tetrafluoroethyl ether (OTE) was used as a diluent to prepare a 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaFSI in AN/OTE electrolyte. After optimizing the electrolyte composition and ratio (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the AN:OTE volume ratio was determined to be 4:6 (abbreviated as 1M-AN/OTE, with a molar ratio of NaFSI: AN: OTE = 1: 7.52: 2.77), where the NaFSI concentration in the AN solvent is 2.5 <span class="smallCaps">m</span>.23 Na nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra indicated that as the NaFSI concentration increased, the peak shifted to a higher field (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3a</a>), suggesting an enhanced shielding effect around Na<sup>+</sup>, whether from the solvent or anion. Compared to the 1<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaFSI in AN electrolyte, partially replacing AN with OTE significantly enhanced the Na<sup>+</sup> shielding effect in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte. This indicates that the diluent strategy can achieve effects similar to those of high-concentration electrolytes, specifically by strengthening the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and the solvent/anions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0030_R_d69773515e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectroscopy provided additional details on the solvation structure. A C≡N stretching band (<i>ν</i><sub>2</sub> mode) at 2258 cm<sup>−1</sup>, derived from free AN molecules,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0023_R_d69773515e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was observed. Upon the introduction of NaFSI, a new peak emerged at 2266 cm<sup>−1</sup>, attributed to Na<sup>+</sup>-solvating AN molecules (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3b</a>). Compared to the 1M-AN electrolyte, the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte exhibited a reduction in free AN molecules and an increase in coordinated solvent molecules. Raman band consisted of three peaks at 740, 745, and 750 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to free anions, contact ion pairs (CIPs), and cation-anion aggregates (AGGs), respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0023_R_d69773515e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the 1M-AN electrolyte was dominated by free FSI<sup>−</sup> anions with only a small presence of CIPs and AGGs, the introduction of the OTE diluent in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte resulted in the complete disappearance of free FSI<sup>−</sup> and a substantial increase in AGGs, comparable to that found in 4M NaFSI in AN (abbreviated as 4M-AN). These results indicate that the partial substitution of AN with OTE can effectively regulate the formation of an anion-rich solvation structure similar to that found in high-concentration electrolytes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bfa98590-1ae2-4fa0-aac5-ec006520303b/aenm202405319-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bfa98590-1ae2-4fa0-aac5-ec006520303b/aenm202405319-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/783d4ba5-7d51-494d-8f78-fbacf26fc157/aenm202405319-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bfa98590-1ae2-4fa0-aac5-ec006520303b/aenm202405319-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405319-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405319" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Solvation structure of the AN-based electrolyte. a) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra, with 0.5 M NaCl in D<sub>2</sub>O as the internal standard. Raman spectra b) in the 2240–2280 cm<sup>−1</sup> range and c) in the 700–760 cm<sup>−1</sup> range. d, g) Molecular dynamics snapshots and magnified views of the local solvation structure, along with e,h) radial distribution functions and coordination numbers. f, i) solvation structure distributions obtained by analyzing 10000 frames of molecular dynamics simulation trajectories. Figures (d, e, f) show results for the 1M-AN electrolyte, while (g, h, i) present results for the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Classical molecular dynamics (cMD) simulations were conducted to further study the solvation structures of the 1M-AN and 1M-AN/OTE electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3d,g</a>). With the partial replacement of AN by the non-coordinating diluent OTE, the movement of FSI<sup>−</sup> and AN was restricted, leading to an enhanced interaction between these species and Na<sup>+</sup>, as indicated by larger g(r) values consistent with the NMR observations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3e,h</a>). No coordination between OTE and Na<sup>+</sup> was observed. By partially replacing AN with OTE, the pairing of Na-N<sub>AN</sub> decreased from 4.81 in the 1M-AN electrolyte to 4.23 in the 1M-AN/OTE system, helping to suppress the reductive decomposition of AN on HC. Additionally, the number of FSI<sup>−</sup> anions in the first solvation shell increased significantly from 0.26 to 0.83 in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte compared to the 1M-AN electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3e,h</a>), aligning well with the results observed in the Raman spectra. Further analysis of molecular dynamics simulation trajectories revealed notable changes in solvation structures. In the 1M-AN electrolyte, the primary solvation structures included four AN molecules coordinated with one Na<sup>+</sup> (32.68%), five AN molecules with one Na<sup>+</sup> (28.2%), three AN molecules with one Na<sup>+</sup> (12.56%), and four AN molecules plus one FSI<sup>−</sup> ion with one Na<sup>+</sup> (8.02%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3f</a>). However, with the partial replacement of AN by OTE in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte, the primary solvation structures shifted to four AN molecules with one Na<sup>+</sup> (19.06%), four AN molecules plus one FSI<sup>−</sup> ion with one Na<sup>+</sup> (16.25%), three AN molecules plus one FSI<sup>−</sup> ion with one Na<sup>+</sup> (14.62%), and five AN molecules with one Na<sup>+</sup> (14.45%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0003">3i</a>). Notably, the probability of two or more FSI<sup>−</sup> ions coordinating around a Na<sup>+</sup> increased from 1.59% in the 1M-AN electrolyte to 17.25% in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). It has been reported that incorporating more anions into the first solvation shell aids in forming a favorable interfacial layer rich in inorganic species,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0028_R_d69773515e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0031_R_d69773515e1031" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thereby enhancing the stability of the HC/electrolyte interface.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0060-title">2.4 Interfacial Stability Assessment</h3>
            
            <p>To determine the impact of changes in the solvation structure on interfacial stability, the differential capacity (<i>dQ/dV</i>) curve of HC during the initial sodiation process was studied, which provides crucial information on the reduction sequence of various electrolyte components. For the HC electrode using the 1M-AN electrolyte, two significant reduction peaks are observed at 0.53 V and 0.89 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4a</a>). These peaks are attributed to the electrochemical reduction and decomposition of FSI<sup>−</sup> and AN, respectively, which is confirmed by controlled experiments as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4b,c</a> presents the trajectory-averaged projected density of states (PDOS) derived from ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations for 1M-AN and 1M-AN/OTE. Notably, the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) of 1M-AN is located at both AN and FSI<sup>−</sup>, indicating their joint involvement in SEI layer formation. In contrast, the LUMO in 1M-AN/OTE is positioned at FSI<sup>−</sup>, suggesting that FSI<sup>−</sup> molecules are preferentially reduced on HC. Consequently, the reduction of AN may be delayed or even suppressed. For the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte, the reduction potential of FSI<sup>−</sup> in the differential capacity curve of HC during the first sodiation is higher, and the reduction peak of AN is almost unobservable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4a</a>). This indicates that the partial replacement of AN with OTE indeed effectively suppresses the reduction and decomposition of AN, potentially inducing the reduction and decomposition of FSI<sup>−</sup> to form a sodium fluoride (NaF)-rich SEI layer.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19004a02-8b8a-4c08-bee9-3d3e025cb1d9/aenm202405319-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/19004a02-8b8a-4c08-bee9-3d3e025cb1d9/aenm202405319-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58df45b7-e0cf-4543-b6f7-5b0e4a7c4d01/aenm202405319-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19004a02-8b8a-4c08-bee9-3d3e025cb1d9/aenm202405319-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405319-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405319" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Interphase composition and stability. a) Differential capacity curves during the initial sodiation of the HC electrode. PDOS of b) the 1M-AN and c) 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte. XPS spectra of the HC surface showing d) S 2p, e) F 1s, f) N 1s, and g) the atomic percentages of C, N, F, and S. h) In situ impedance evolution of the HC electrode during cycling and i) corresponding DRT analysis curves.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was conducted to examine the SEI components on the HC surface. For the electrolyte cycled in 1M-AN/OTE, sulfur-containing species such as, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e62198e8-5da4-4be1-a26f-0e55dd206a5c/aenm202405319-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405319:aenm202405319-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202405319-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                           <mo>,</mo>
                        </mrow>
                        <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                        <msubsup>
                           <mi>SO</mi>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </mrow>
                        </msubsup>
                        <mo>,</mo>
                        <msubsup>
                           <mi>SO</mi>
                           <mn>4</mn>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </mrow>
                        </msubsup>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{N}}{{\mathrm{a}}_{\mathrm{2}}}{\mathrm{S,}}\;{\mathrm{SO}}_3^{2 - }, {\mathrm{SO}}_4^{2 - }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> generated by the decomposition of FSI<sup>−</sup> entering the first solvation layer can be observed in the S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0020_R_d69773515e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0023_R_d69773515e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the HC electrode cycled in 1M-AN electrolyte only shows a single signal in the S 2p spectrum, with an atomic sulfur content of only 13%, significantly lower than the 20% observed in 1M-AN/OTE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4g</a>), indicating limited decomposition of FSI<sup>−</sup>. In the F 1s spectrum, a peak ≈685.5 eV can be attributed to NaF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4e</a>), which is considered an excellent SEI component due to its good electronic insulation properties and ability to effectively inhibit continuous side reactions between HC and the electrolyte, originating from the reduction and decomposition of NaFSI. The F content in the SEI derived from the 1M-AN electrolyte is only 34%, which failed to form a robust SEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4g</a>). Notably, an additional S-F signal was observed in the 1M-AN/OTE group, indicating that more NaFSI entered the primary solvation shell, underwent reductive decomposition, and contributed to SEI formation. This observation is consistent with the results from Raman spectroscopy and molecular dynamics, with the F content in the SEI layer derived from 1M-AN/OTE reaching 46%, far exceeding that of its 1M-AN counterpart, suggesting the formation of a NaF-rich SEI layer. In the N 1s spectrum, a significant C≡N signal is observed for the 1M-AN group,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0032_R_d69773515e1152" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> attributed to the extensive decomposition of AN, as also evidenced by the higher C atomic content (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4f</a>). The peak ≈399.5 eV can be attributed to Na<sub>3</sub>N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4f</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0032_R_d69773515e1166" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0033_R_d69773515e1166" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is a beneficial SEI component with a low Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier, favorable for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The stability of the robust SEI layer derived from 1M-AN/OTE can be further elucidated by in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). Specifically, after 78 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;C, no significant changes in interfacial resistance are observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4h</a>). DRT analysis further confirmed the exceptional stability of the SEI derived from the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte, as evidenced by the near-perfect overlap of 26 DRT curves and the absence of any time-constant shifts or impedance growth with cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0004">4i</a>). This stands in sharp contrast to the 1M-AN electrolyte, where both impedance and time constants continually increased (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2f</a>). In addition, the SEI layer on HC exhibits a uniform and thin morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), further demonstrating that the SEI derived from 1M-AN/OTE effectively suppresses interfacial side reactions and undesired excessive growth. As previously mentioned, the participation of more anions in forming a robust, F- and S-rich inorganic layer is precisely what endows this stable SEI with its resistance to growth over repeated cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0020_R_d69773515e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202405319-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405319-bib-0028_R_d69773515e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Energy Lett. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0070-title">2.5 Performance Verification of Optimized Electrolyte</h3>
            
            <p>The rate performance of HC in the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte was evaluated using a Na metal-free three-electrode setup. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5a</a>, HC exhibited significantly improved rate performance in the optimized 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte compared to the 1M-AN electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0002">2a</a>). Specifically, it delivered 258 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;C, achieving 86.3% of its 0.2&nbsp;C capacity (299 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and 223 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5C, retaining 74.6% capacity. This enhancement is attributed to the formation of a robust SEI film rich in inorganic components, which effectively reduced polarization and notably increased the plateau capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The HC||NVP full cell with 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte exhibits outstanding rate performance, with minimal capacity decay during charge/discharge testing up to 15&nbsp;C, retaining 91.2% of its 1&nbsp;C capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4ec967e8-945d-47a6-b538-fd49d6608a66/aenm202405319-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4ec967e8-945d-47a6-b538-fd49d6608a66/aenm202405319-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/17d87046-1af7-4464-8227-a2a36c43ebd8/aenm202405319-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4ec967e8-945d-47a6-b538-fd49d6608a66/aenm202405319-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405319-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405319" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the optimized AN-based electrolyte. a) The rate performance of HC was obtained using a Na-metal-free three-electrode configuration. b) Charge and discharge curves at different rates. c) Rate performance of the HC||NVP full cell. d) Long-term cycling performance at 5&nbsp;C and e) low-temperature performance of the HC||NVP full cell. f) Long-term cycling performance of the HC||NFM full cell at 5&nbsp;C with a cut-off voltage of 4.15 V. g) Performance comparison of HC||NFM full cells reported in the past five years.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cycling performance of the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte was assessed in HC||NVP full cells. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte demonstrated clear advantages over other electrolytes at both high and low rates. Specifically, at 5&nbsp;C, the capacity retention was as high as 79.1% after 2500 charge/discharge cycles. In contrast, due to repeated Na plating and deactivation, full cells using carbonate-based electrolytes retained only 8.0% of their capacity after 2500 cycles. While ether-based electrolytes (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME, denoted as NP035) allow fast charge/discharge of HC, they are unable to withstand high voltages, retaining only 39.9% of their capacity. Moreover, the full cell with the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte retained 73.9% of its capacity after 1000 charge/discharge cycles at 1&nbsp;C, significantly outperforming conventional carbonate and ether-based electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, it was pleasantly surprising to find that partially replacing AN with OTE broadens the liquid temperature range of the AN-based electrolyte. Compared to the 1M-AN electrolyte, the AN/OTE electrolyte shows no significant exothermic peaks from −75 to 0&nbsp;°C, and the endothermic peak at 150&nbsp;°C is greatly diminished (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). This improvement enables full cells using the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte to perform exceptionally well even under extreme temperature conditions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5e</a>, the HC||NVP battery delivered 89.0% of its room-temperature capacity when charged and discharged at −20&nbsp;°C and 77.7% at −30&nbsp;°C. Under cycling at 60&nbsp;°C, the capacity retention after 500 cycles reached 65.0%, surpassing the 52.9% retention of traditional carbonate-based electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the inherent high oxidative stability of nitrile solvents and their resistance to aluminum foil corrosion enable the designed electrolyte to be compatible with high-voltage cathodes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Specifically, the HC||NFM full cell using the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte demonstrated stable cycling for 1400 cycles at a high cut-off voltage of 4.15 V and a rate of 5&nbsp;C, achieving a capacity retention of 82.0% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5f</a>). In addition, the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte can also support cycling with thick electrodes (3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0005">5g</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the 1M-AN/OTE electrolyte endows the HC||NFM full cell with highly competitive performance under high cut-off voltages (i.e., 4 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na and above).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405319-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405319-sec-0080-title">2.6 Practical and Versatile Sodium-Ion Batteries</h3>
            
            <p>Finally, the practicality of this electrolyte was further validated with a 3Ah pouch cell (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0006">6a</a>, the HC||NFM pouch cell (with an energy density of 132.2 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of the pouch cell, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) maintains a capacity of 2896 mAh after 1000 charge–discharge cycles at 1&nbsp;C in constant current-constant voltage (CC-CV) mode, achieving an impressive capacity retention rate of 90.7%. On top of that, it retains 95.8% capacity even at a 3&nbsp;C charge-discharge rate compared to 1&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0006">6b</a>). Additionally, the pouch cell exhibited excellent performance in extremely low-temperature environments, retaining 81.0% of its room-temperature capacity at −40&nbsp;°C and 56.4% at −60&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0006">6c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the exceptionally stable electrode-electrolyte interface allows the pouch cell to operate reliably even at elevated temperatures of 50&nbsp;°C, maintaining a high capacity retention rate of 97.3% after 350 cycles at 1&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0006">6d</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405319-fig-0006">6e</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) compare the performance of practical pouch-type HC-based SIBs modified by electrolytes, as reported in the past five years. The results indicate that the electrolyte modification strategy using 1M-AN/OTE developed in this work holds significant promise.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405319-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/74d0271a-bba7-4be9-a129-d92c071d77ba/aenm202405319-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/74d0271a-bba7-4be9-a129-d92c071d77ba/aenm202405319-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a6e733e5-54af-43b3-9bfe-070d3b0b5d66/aenm202405319-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/74d0271a-bba7-4be9-a129-d92c071d77ba/aenm202405319-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
